  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);

  <span id="freeText17650760598538929922" style="display:none">On the empty winter prairie, gray clouds to the northwest meant only one thing: a blizzard was seconds away. The first blizzard came in October. It snowed almost without stopping until April. The temperature dropped to forty below. Snow reached the roof-tops. And no trains could get through with food and coal. The townspeople began to starve. The Ingalls family barely lived through that winter. And Almanzo Wilder knew he would have to risk his life to save the town.</span>

<a href="/questions/1557186-for-those-of-you-living-in-the-dakotas">For those of you living in the Dakotas, can winter really be this bad?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_1557186'><a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F8282.The_Long_Winter">like</a></span>

<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/8742774-elizabeth"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1334251979i/8742774._UY100_CR5,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/8742774-elizabeth">Elizabeth</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I'm not in the Dakotas, but yes, the research says that Laura's first winter was as awful as described. It's called the long winter throughout the Uni<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I'm not in the Dakotas, but yes, the research says that Laura's first winter was as awful as described. It's called the long winter throughout the United States. My understanding is there was a short growing season the following summer, and so the next winter, while not full of blizzards, was devastating as well.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=newbery">newbery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1729042596" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13764002218320887465">This place is a double Hell Hole, compared to Plum Creek and its crickets. No amount of Pa's fiddle-playing can compensate for the fact that they all almost died of hunger and cold during this winter. <br/><br/>Remind me again, what was so bad about Big Woods in book one? </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2134677134" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2134677134" />
    <a title="Miranda Reads" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/71848701-miranda-reads"><img alt="Miranda Reads" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592243041p2/71848701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2134677134?book_show_action=true">Sep 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Miranda Reads" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Miranda Reads" href="/user/show/71848701-miranda-reads">Miranda Reads</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780064400060" href="/book/show/538300.The_Long_Winter">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71848701-miranda-reads?shelf=middlegrade">middlegrade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71848701-miranda-reads?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2134677134" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15070886668055818960"><b>I would have died ten times over if I lived during Laura's time</b><br/><br/>The whole family moves into town to weather the winter of 1800-1801 - and it's good thing they do. This was one of the harshest winters they would ever face. <b>Snow soon piles over their windows</b> and the bitter cold ensures that they cannot leave their houses. Their fuel runs out, <b>their food consists of scraps,</b> and Pa can no longer play the fiddle for his hands are stiff with cold.<br/><br/>Even in her toughest year, faced with bitter cold and st</span>
  <span id="freeText15070886668055818960" style="display:none"><b>I would have died ten times over if I lived during Laura's time</b><br /><br />The whole family moves into town to weather the winter of 1800-1801 - and it's good thing they do. This was one of the harshest winters they would ever face. <b>Snow soon piles over their windows</b> and the bitter cold ensures that they cannot leave their houses. Their fuel runs out, <b>their food consists of scraps,</b> and Pa can no longer play the fiddle for his hands are stiff with cold.<br /><br />Even in her toughest year, faced with bitter cold and starvation, Laura still conveys the beauty of the prairie. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>Then the sun peeped over the edge of the prairie and the whole world glittered. Every tiniest thing glittered rosy toward the sun and pale blue toward the sky, and all along every blade of grass ran rainbow sparkles.</i>
</blockquote><br />We are introduced to Almanzo as an adult. (The first time since <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1079587.Farmer_Boy__Little_House___3_" title="Farmer Boy (Little House, #3) by Laura Ingalls Wilder" rel="nofollow noopener">Farmer Boy</a>.) Laura (in the story) admires him first for his horses, <b>then for his kindness </b>and then for his bravery as he hitches up his team of horses to make a run for fuel for the entire town. I appreciate that he remains as a background character. <b>Their love plays out so slowly</b> compared to many teen books that it has time to bloom and blossom.<br /><br />Wholesomely inspirational and heartwarming. <b>An excellent book to read curled up under a blanket with a cup of cocoa. </b><br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“It can't beat us!" Pa said.<br />"Can't it, Pa?" Laura asked stupidly.<br />"No," said Pa. "It's got to quit sometime and we don't. It can't lick us. We won't give up."</i>
</blockquote><b>Audiobook Comments</b><br />Read by Cherry Jones and accompanied by Paul Woodiel on the fiddle. Such an incredible audiobook.<br /><br /><a href="http://www.mirandareads.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.instagram.com/miranda.reads/?hl=en" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/Miranda_Reads" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="15070886668055818960" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1137464282" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1137464282" />
    <a title="Diane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/279256-diane"><img alt="Diane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1497365060p2/279256.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1137464282?book_show_action=true">Dec 21, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane" href="/user/show/279256-diane">Diane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780064400060" href="/book/show/538300.The_Long_Winter">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=pioneers">pioneers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=midwest">midwest</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1137464282" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1025430970276410106">It was fitting that I read "The Long Winter" while visiting family in Minnesota. It was bitterly cold, the streets were packed with snow and the wind chill was below zero. As I read, I could hear the wind howling outside, and the harsh winter of 1880-81 didn't seem like that long ago.<br/><br/>Book six in the Little House series tells how the Ingalls family survived numerous blizzards while homesteading near De Smet, South Dakota. Pa first sensed that the season would be severe when he was harvesting hay </span>
  <span id="freeText1025430970276410106" style="display:none">It was fitting that I read "The Long Winter" while visiting family in Minnesota. It was bitterly cold, the streets were packed with snow and the wind chill was below zero. As I read, I could hear the wind howling outside, and the harsh winter of 1880-81 didn't seem like that long ago.<br /><br />Book six in the Little House series tells how the Ingalls family survived numerous blizzards while homesteading near De Smet, South Dakota. Pa first sensed that the season would be severe when he was harvesting hay and he saw the thick mud walls of a muskrat house.<br /><br /><blockquote><br />Pa was shaking his head. "We're going to have a hard winter," he said, not liking the prospect.<br /><br />"Why, how do you know?" Laura asked in surprise.<br /><br />"The colder the winter will be, the thicker the muskrats build the walls of their houses," Pa told her. "I never saw a heavier-built muskrats' house than that one."<br /></blockquote><br /><br />A few weeks later, a wise old Indian stopped by the town's store to warn the white folks about winter. He said there would be heavy snow and strong winds for seven months. Indeed, that winter brought many long blizzards, and with each one, the town's supplies went down. All of the animals had fled the area, so hunting was scarce, and the snow was so deep that the train couldn't get through to deliver food or coal. <br /><br />(While reading this, I remembered that the closest thing we currently have to scarcity in winter is when the local store runs out of bread and milk for a day because of a panic over snow.)<br /><br />Like the others in the series, this book has good reminders about just how <i>hard</i> homesteading was. Pa and the other pioneers worked long hours to get the fields ready for crops, and they had to build everything from scratch. When the family ran out of coal to burn for heat, Pa figured out a way to twist hay into sticks, so they could burn that. When they ran out of kerosene, Ma figured out how to make a "button candle" using axle grease.<br /><br /><blockquote><br />"We didn't lack for light when I was a girl, before this newfangled kerosene was ever heard of."<br /><br />"That's so," said Pa. "These times are too progressive. Everything has changed too fast. Railroads and telegraph and kerosene and coal stoves — they're good things to have but the trouble is, folks get to depend on 'em."<br /></blockquote><br /><br />And when there wasn't any wheat or flour left in town, well, luckily Almanzo Wilder had the courage to go and try to find some more.<br /><br />Any Little House fans reading this will perk up at the name of Almanzo, because that is who Laura will eventually marry. This book is the first one where Laura seems to notice him, which was sweet.<br /><br />I think the purpose of this book was to show how dangerous those prairie winters were. Neighbors had to work together and help each other to survive. In the modern, self-involved age we live in, this story was a reminder of how a small town used to be.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1025430970276410106" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_59900968" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/59900968" />
    <a title="Hannah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2411388-hannah"><img alt="Hannah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1272561978p2/2411388.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/59900968?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hannah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hannah" href="/user/show/2411388-hannah">Hannah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060581855" href="/book/show/1032763.The_Long_Winter">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2411388-hannah?shelf=2012-reads">2012-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2411388-hannah?shelf=comfort-books">comfort-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2411388-hannah?shelf=2010-reads">2010-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2411388-hannah?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2411388-hannah?shelf=childhood-favs">childhood-favs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer59900968" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4618446104958997284">Gah, I love the Little House books, and none more than <i>The Long Winter</i>, the 6th in the series.<br/><br/>Although all of Laura Ingalls' books have a cozy, homey charm, <i>The Long Winter</i> brings with it a gritier, more menacing realism of what life would actually have been like for the American pioneer. Since it is a children's book, Laura keeps the threat light, but make no mistake, the threat of starvation is a serious and ever present danger to not only the Ingalls family, but all the residents of De Smet, </span>
  <span id="freeText4618446104958997284" style="display:none">Gah, I love the Little House books, and none more than <i>The Long Winter</i>, the 6th in the series.<br><br>Although all of Laura Ingalls' books have a cozy, homey charm, <i>The Long Winter</i> brings with it a gritier, more menacing realism of what life would actually have been like for the American pioneer. Since it is a children's book, Laura keeps the threat light, but make no mistake, the threat of starvation is a serious and ever present danger to not only the Ingalls family, but all the residents of De Smet, SD in that winter of continual blizzards.<br><br>This is the time when you can really see the strength of the family Ingalls. Although I have no doubt that Laura wrote this with the aid of some time-tinted, rosy glasses and a due respect for her young reading audience, I also see that she didn't shy away from showing us how the constant struggle to keep warm, keep fed, and keep mentally strong wasn't pretty and wasn't easy for any of them. Pa's half-crazed tongue lashing at the blizzard, gentle ma's sharp rebuke of her beloved husband, and Laura's fearful initiation into the responsibilities of adulthood all make for riveting reading, regardless of your age.<br><br>I love this family, and never more so then in this book. This series, more then any other I can think of, makes me proud of my American heritage and the strength of its earlier settlers. We could all stand to have more of their type of strength and perserverence today.<br><br><br><br>***<br>2012 personal reading challenge for the month of January:<br><b>
  <i>Childhood favorites that influenced my subsequent reading life</i>
</b><br><br>Adult Equivalent:<br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/537375.The_Endurance_Shackleton_s_Legendary_Antarctic_Expedition" title="The Endurance Shackleton's Legendary Antarctic Expedition by Caroline Alexander" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://s3.amazonaws.com/photo.goodreads.com/books/1320487170s/537375.jpg" alt="The Endurance Shackleton's Legendary Antarctic Expedition by Caroline Alexander" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><i>The Endurance</i> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/59027.Caroline_Alexander" title="Caroline Alexander" rel="nofollow noopener">Caroline Alexander</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="4618446104958997284" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1343961418" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1343961418" />
    <a title="Elizabeth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/717971-elizabeth"><img alt="Elizabeth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1207771377p2/717971.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1343961418?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth" href="/user/show/717971-elizabeth">Elizabeth</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780060264604" href="/book/show/776984.The_Long_Winter">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer1343961418&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1343961418" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer283609272655248389">The Long Review		<br/><br/><br/>Again, I get <i>literal chills</i> reading even just the beginning of this book – how high and fast the geese fly, knowing instinctively the freakishly hard winter that lies ahead, how the whole country lies empty of wildlife long before usual, the muskrats asleep deep in their thick mud house.<br/><br/>I actually think that I learned everything I know about foreshadowing from <i>The Long Winter</i> (and probably everything I know about cold and hunger). The novel’s leisurely, foreboding set-up – the h</span>
  <span id="freeText283609272655248389" style="display:none">The Long Review		<br /><br /><br />Again, I get <i>literal chills</i> reading even just the beginning of this book – how high and fast the geese fly, knowing instinctively the freakishly hard winter that lies ahead, how the whole country lies empty of wildlife long before usual, the muskrats asleep deep in their thick mud house.<br /><br />I actually think that I learned everything I know about foreshadowing from <i>The Long Winter</i> (and probably everything I know about cold and hunger). The novel’s leisurely, foreboding set-up – the heat of the haying, the meagre harvest, the muskrats’ deep building and the wild birds instinctive flight – then the old Native American stranger warning that he’s seen this before – Almanzo’s first appearance, casually offering directions when Carrie and Laura are lost in the Big Slough – blissfully unaware, as he lazes in the sun, how fiercely he’ll be tested and proven by deathly cold and those same long grasses at the climax of the novel, when he risks his life to feed the town. I <i>swear</i> this is the way I build a story, and I learned it here.<br /><br />I hear echoes in my own work, too, of theme and structure – here’s Laura, drawing well water as the sun rises over a world encased in hard frost, thinking:<br /><br /><i>Laura loved the beautiful world. She knew that the bitter frost had killed the hay and the garden. The tangled tomato vines with their red and green tomatoes, and the pumpkin vines holding their broad leaves over the green young pumpkins, were all glittering bright in frost over the broken, frosty sod. The sod corn’s stalks and long leaves were white. The frost had killed them. It would leave every living green thing dead. But the frost was beautiful.</i><br /><br />And it reminds me <i>so much</i> of my own character Rose flying through the war-torn sky over Kent, though I was unconscious of the similarity as I wrote this passage from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17262236.Rose_Under_Fire__Code_Name_Verity___2_" title="Rose Under Fire (Code Name Verity, #2) by Elizabeth Wein" rel="nofollow noopener">Rose Under Fire</a>:<br /><br /><i>I can’t get over how beautiful the barrage balloons are. I can’t even talk about it to anyone - they all think I am crazy. But when you’re flying over them, and the sky above you is a sea of gray mist and the land below you is all green, the silver balloons float in between like a school of shining silver whales, bobbing a little in the wind. They are as big as buses, and me and every other pilot has a healthy fear of them because their tethering cables are all loaded with explosives to try to snarl up enemy aircraft. But they are just magical from above, great big silver bubbles filling the sky.<br /><br />Incredible. It is just </i>incredible<i> that you can notice something like this when your face is so cold you can’t feel it any more, and you know perfectly well you are surrounded by </i>death<i> and the only way to stay alive is to endure the howling wind and stay on course. And </i>still<i> the sky is beautiful.</i><br /><br />And both passages are fearsome foreshadowing for the life-and-death struggles that lie in wait for both girls later in their respective books. (In some ways, <i>Rose Under Fire</i> is DEEPLY influenced by <i>The Long Winter</i>.)<br /><br />Going back to Wilder’s expert foreshadowing – I must touch on the early blizzards. An October blizzard hits the family while they are living in their shanty on their claim, and though they have plenty of food and hunger is a non-issue, the storm serves to warn them that they cannot survive the winter in this unfinished house, prompting their life-saving move to town. The second early blizzard, after the move, serves the plot in a ton of ways. The storm hits during school hours, so forces Carrie and Laura out into its teeth so we get to experience the terror of snow-blindness, banshee winds and “zero weather” <i>through Laura’s eyes</i>; it is this storm that first forces the town into isolation, eventually making it too dangerous to keep school open for the winter; but most important of all, hugely important, it introduces us to Cap Garland, Almanzo’s partner-in-crime in the heroic journey for wheat that is the novel’s climax. Cap is youthful and charismatic, Laura’s schoolmate, but he’s also the only one who recognizes the danger the school group is in as they stumble through the storm, and he takes action to save them. So right here in the beginning of the book we know he’s intelligent, capable, brave, and self-sacrificing. And this chapter is actually <i>called</i> “Cap Garland,” not “Lost in the Storm” or “Blizzard at School” or any number of other things the chapter heading might highlight. It highlights Cap himself.<br /><br />This book is absolutely beautifully structured, and I can’t ever read it now without noticing it. Even Laura and Carrie’s trip down Main Street to buy Pa’s Christmas present serves a dual purpose – it gives the reader a chance to see through Laura’s eyes the emptiness of both grocery stores as the town waits anxiously for a supply train to get through. The frightening wildness of each blizzard’s arrival is heightened now by the knowledge that every new storm sets back the desperately-needed train’s arrival by another day. There is no action or passage wasted anywhere in this text: it all serves to drive the plot, often in more than one way. <br /><br />Almanzo’s point of view is woven throughout the book so that the reader is used to seeing things from his point of view when the climax comes, but what he’s thinking is also important as it builds tension as he tries to save his own wheat. Eventually Laura’s and Almanzo’s storylines come together: it is Pa’s discovery of Almanzo’s hidden seed wheat that makes Almanzo realize there are people in the town who are literally in danger of starving to death, and precipitates his quest. (I like the way Pa is “Pa” in the text when the third person narrator is in Laura’s head, but he is “Mr. Ingalls” when the narrator is in Almanzo’s head.)<br /><br />And then the <i>suspense</i>, my <i>God</i> - in the chapter “For Daily Bread” she details the whole trip in the freezing cold as Cap and Almanzo find the mythic wheat, but they are in danger of losing their feet to frostbite on the journey back, the horses keep floundering in deep snow, they are never sure of their location, and just as the sun is setting and the next blizzard cloud is swirling in from the northwest, the chapter ends, and WE CUT TO A WHOLE CHAPTER BACK IN THE COLD, FOODLESS DARK INGALLS HOUSE.<br /><br />It’s masterful.<br /><br />In the last chapter, “Christmas in May” (I think – the book is out of my hands at the moment) the Boasts come and share “Christmas” dinner with the family, and of course that Pa is able to play his fiddle again – order and sanity is restored, all these hugely important things they have been deprived of all winter, social interaction and music and warmth and color, are back in their world. But I also really love that basically this chapter is just an orgy of food. The whole Christmas dinner is described in exquisite detail including scent and taste and color and texture, as they prepare it and eat it and have second and third helpings.<br /><br /><i>Praise God for bread.</i><br /><br />---------------------<br /><br />On the end paper, in the back of my copy, handwritten in pencil, is the toll free number to report power outages to the local electric company in Central Pennsylvania. My grandmother and I read the whole book aloud to each other in three days, snowbound during the blizzard of March 1993. I love this obscure memento.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="283609272655248389" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_661163572" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/661163572" />
    <a title="Philip" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/71601-philip"><img alt="Philip" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587450863p2/71601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/661163572?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Philip" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Philip" href="/user/show/71601-philip">Philip</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780064400060" href="/book/show/538300.The_Long_Winter">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71601-philip?shelf=eleanor-review">eleanor-review</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71601-philip?shelf=children">children</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71601-philip?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71601-philip?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer661163572" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11997454258579673235">Eleanor and I just finished this up last night. A couple thoughts before she starts her review:<br/><br/>I saw a facebook post not too long ago in which the person was opining that they didn't live in the "Little House days." This was in regard to Christmas. They mentioned how Laura and Mary et al received only one or two presents and were thrilled and grateful to receive them. You know, that was a "simpler time."<br/><br/>Several days later, I saw that they were taking a trip to Disney for Christmas. And there wer</span>
  <span id="freeText11997454258579673235" style="display:none">Eleanor and I just finished this up last night. A couple thoughts before she starts her review:<br /><br />I saw a facebook post not too long ago in which the person was opining that they didn't live in the "Little House days." This was in regard to Christmas. They mentioned how Laura and Mary et al received only one or two presents and were thrilled and grateful to receive them. You know, that was a "simpler time."<br /><br />Several days later, I saw that they were taking a trip to Disney for Christmas. And there were no shortage of packages under their tree.<br /><br />Not that I begrudge them going to Disney. I love that place. (Although, not as much as my sister... She takes it to a whole new level.)<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8282.The_Long_Winter__Little_House___6_" title="The Long Winter (Little House, #6) by Laura Ingalls Wilder" rel="nofollow noopener">The Long Winter</a>, though, makes it pretty clear that we're romanticizing those times. The times were simpler - if you equate simple with hunger, boredom, and intense manual labor.<br /><br />Not only that, but in certain circles, the Wilders are elevated to a Protestant sainthood. (Ok... most Protestant denominations already believe in the "sainthood of the believer," but lets set that aside for a moment.) The Wilder's are often put on a pedestal. That every decision they made was a right and moral one. (As they were living in right and moral times - as oppose to today's immoral, consumer-driven, drugged-out, gunned-out, society.)<br /><br />But throughout the book, I saw them as people - people who made mistakes in very, <i>very</i> difficult times. There was a mob-mentality at the store when they forced Loftus to "see reason" and sell the goods at a reasonable price. True, Loftus agreed - and he shouldn't have jacked the price up in the first place - but I'm not sure that justifies what the town was doing. Or stealing from the emigrant train car. <i>"'I'm past caring what he ought to do!' Pa said savagely. 'Let the railroad stand some damages! This isn't the only family in town that's got nothing to eat. We told Woodworth to open up that car or we'd do it. He tried to argue that there'll be another train tomorrow, but we didn't feel like waiting...'"</i><br /><br />As a society, we can understand some forms of stealing - but that doesn't absolve the thief from the law.<br /><br />Consider too, that this is what Laura wrote. (She wouldn't have been writing all the faults of her father in a children's book.) No doubt, the Ingalls were great people - but they were people. They were struggling to survive. They did what they could with little complaining - and that is admirable. But they were people living in a difficult time, with their share of mistakes and sins. In that way, they are very similar to all of us.<br /><br />I have to look into getting Eleanor a goodreads account of her own. She's been sitting here patiently waiting for me to type up my part before she gets her say. She's got the patience of an Ingalls.<br /><br />Eleanor: I've been thinking about my favorite part.<br /><br />Dad: And did you come up with one?<br /><br />E: YEAH!<br /><br />D: What is it?<br /><br />E: My favorite part was when they made hay while the sun shines.<br /><br />D: Why was that your favorite part?<br /><br />E: Because it was before the winter, and it was so nice and warm outside the claim shanty, and they didn't move into the town yet, and it was just so warm in their house.<br /><br />D: So you didn't like the winter then?<br /><br />E: No. It was too cold. Heh. WAAAAAAAAYYYYYY to cold. Way way way way way way way too cold.<br /><br />D: So you didn't like most of the book then.<br /><br />E: Well, I like the book. I just didn't like that it was so cold for so long for them. Because we usually have winters from December to February.<br /><br />D: That's a good point.<br /><br />E: Can you put a smiley face at the end?<br /><br />D: Why?<br /><br />E: At the end of the sentence. BECAUSE I'M SMILING!!! :)<br /><br />D: Ok. I'll put it at the end of the sentence you just said. Tell me more about The Long Winter. What you'd think about it?<br /><br />E: Pretty good. They were shivering, and they couldn't feel their feet. And they were selling wheat.<br /><br />D: What was the book about?<br /><br />E: KITTY! It wasn't about the kitty. I just saw the kitty and it distracted me. It was about when they settled in town for the hard winter. They were cold. And every day they shivered. And they only had brown bread and potatoes. Sometimes they had cod-fish gravy. Laura and Pa were always twisting hay into sticks for the fire.<br /><br />D: Do you think it would be fun to live back then, or are you happy to live now?<br /><br />E: Ummmmm... Either way is ok.<br /><br />D: Why's that?<br /><br />E: Back then they didn't have electricity. But I wonder how that brown bread tastes, and I didn't have cod-fish gravy.<br /><br />D: You've had brown bread before.<br /><br />E: When?<br /><br />D: I think we have some downstairs right now. Although, I think it's softer than the bread Mary and Laura ate.<br /><br />E: Why?<br /><br />D: Because of the way it's made. ...So, if you had to choose - when would you live?<br /><br />E: Now.<br /><br />D: Me too. But, really, I think either would be ok.<br /><br />E: Me too.<br /><br />D: Should I put anything else in here?<br /><br />E: YEAH! THEN IT WAS SPRING AND THE CHINOOK WAS BLOWING!!!!<br /><br />D: I think that's the moral of the story.<br /><br />E: What's "moral" mean? The "end" of the story?<br /><br />D: No, it means the message, or the point of the story. It means, that sometimes times are tough. But spring, and the good times have to come eventually - if you can outlast the bad.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11997454258579673235" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer15301935714602136490">While this is not the most compelling Little House book it is a very important part of the story. I cannot imagine a better<br/>character building book. No, it is not compelling to be confronted with one's own weaknesses</span>
  <span id="freeText15301935714602136490" style="display:none">While this is not the most compelling Little House book it is a very important part of the story. I cannot imagine a better<br />character building book. To live with the Ingalls through the long winter puts much of life's little frustrations in perspective. <br />When Laura says, "For shame, Grace," after months and months of suffering, and little Grace utters the first and last complaint of the whole book, belies our own time and culture. No, it is not compelling to be confronted with one's own weaknesses, but this book is a vividly drawn picture of a life lived with gratefulness.</span>

My job is considered "emergency personnel " so regardless of weather conditions I am expected to make my appearance. Laura suffered through SEVEN MONTHS of blizzards. Holy Christ! There was some serious deprivation happening in this small town of about 87 people. Wheat bread and potatoes with tea were the rations. I can currently claim multigrain bread and tons of tea as staples in my apartment, not much else. I eat Panera a lot. And smoothie king. How am I supposed to do that in a major snow incident???? I'm thinking I should start storing some provisions-like ramen noodles and canned tomato soup. Please God never ever let me live through a winter like this. I don't think I can have the perseverance and presence of mind these pioneers had, if for no other reason than I can't stand freezing to death. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9614705506128262716">Ever since I first read this series at the age of 9 or so, THIS one stuck out in my memory as a favorite. It just seemed so much more REAL than the others, even if, yes, they are all REAL stories. The Long Winter was indeed that, with 7 months of blizzards nearly freezing and starving the Ingalls family to death. As a kid, I liked it for the adventure of it all, as an adult I like it for the sense of realism- they actually nearly died! Starving, eating crushed up wheat, burning sticks of horse's</span>
  <span id="freeText9614705506128262716" style="display:none">Ever since I first read this series at the age of 9 or so, THIS one stuck out in my memory as a favorite. It just seemed so much more REAL than the others, even if, yes, they are all REAL stories. The Long Winter was indeed that, with 7 months of blizzards nearly freezing and starving the Ingalls family to death. As a kid, I liked it for the adventure of it all, as an adult I like it for the sense of realism- they actually nearly died! Starving, eating crushed up wheat, burning sticks of horse's hay to live, it gives one a sense of how hard live truly could be during those times. Also, I love that ALMANZO is a majo player in this book, and I love the Cap Garland character as well. This is by far, my favorite of the series, with good reason, I think! -Jen from Quebec :0)</span>

<div id="review_513865707" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/513865707" />
    <a title="Kathryn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/446464-kathryn"><img alt="Kathryn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316545868p2/446464.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/513865707?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathryn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathryn" href="/user/show/446464-kathryn">Kathryn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780064400060" href="/book/show/538300.The_Long_Winter">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/446464-kathryn?shelf=very-favorites">very-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/446464-kathryn?shelf=winter">winter</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/446464-kathryn?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/446464-kathryn?shelf=friends-of-my-youth">friends-of-my-youth</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer513865707" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8389690752334521948">Remarkable how Laura is able to write a captivating, moving novel essentially about being housebound for six months during a long, harsh winter of blizzards. Perhaps more remarkable, she is able to convey the drudgery, the monotony, the physical and emotional toll of those dark days without the book becoming a horror story or pity party. For example, moment they realize Pa can no longer play the fiddle because his fingers are too numb and tattered from the cold is utterly heartbreaking (the fidd</span>
  <span id="freeText8389690752334521948" style="display:none">Remarkable how Laura is able to write a captivating, moving novel essentially about being housebound for six months during a long, harsh winter of blizzards. Perhaps more remarkable, she is able to convey the drudgery, the monotony, the physical and emotional toll of those dark days without the book becoming a horror story or pity party. For example, moment they realize Pa can no longer play the fiddle because his fingers are too numb and tattered from the cold is utterly heartbreaking (the fiddle music has helped them through tough times before) but somehow the Ingalls family finds strength within the love of their family and by keeping their Faith and their story is truly inspiring. How vividly I remember, even from my childhood when my mom read this to me, how Laura had to twist hay into “logs” to burn day in and day out, while Ma and the other girls huddled around the meager warmth of the stove, someone turning the coffee grinder every moment to produce the wee bit of flour from the seed wheat so that they did not completely starve. The story is also a terrific springboard for some thoughtful conversation with young people about morals and how people act during harrowing times (I’m thinking here especially about the chapters involving the stored seed wheat). Five bright, shining stars for this one!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8389690752334521948" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2453602484" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2453602484" />
    <a title="Celeste" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7317186-celeste"><img alt="Celeste" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1481055226p2/7317186.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2453602484?book_show_action=true">Jul 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Celeste" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Celeste" href="/user/show/7317186-celeste">Celeste</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7317186-celeste?shelf=childrens-books">childrens-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7317186-celeste?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7317186-celeste?shelf=classics-i-ve-read">classics-i-ve-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2453602484" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11165964461503894995"><b>We live in troubled times.</b> There is civil unrest and prejudice and unwarranted hatred plaguing our world, across borders and oceans and digital platforms. It’s easy to wish we could go back to simpler times, to an era where a man’s word was good and pollution was decades into the future. <br/><br/>But I have to tell you: <b>nothing and nobody and not any amount of money could convince me to travel back in time to trade lives with Laura Ingalls Wilder.</b>. Nope. I love, no, I <i>adore</i> Little House on the Prairie. T</span>
  <span id="freeText11165964461503894995" style="display:none"><b>We live in troubled times.</b> There is civil unrest and prejudice and unwarranted hatred plaguing our world, across borders and oceans and digital platforms. It’s easy to wish we could go back to simpler times, to an era where a man’s word was good and pollution was decades into the future. <br /><br />But I have to tell you: <b>nothing and nobody and not any amount of money could convince me to travel back in time to trade lives with Laura Ingalls Wilder.</b>. Nope. I love, no, I <i>adore</i> Little House on the Prairie. The television series from the 70s has been my happy place all summer. But as the series progresses and as I’ve gotten farther into the books, I’ve come to see the dark side of prairie life. Yes, this was a time before technology made our world smaller and gave us the anonymity to attack those who disagree with us with no concern about the consequences. It was a time when neighbors helped each other, and when man had room to breathe without the crowding that is so common today. <br /><br /><u>But it was also a time of extreme hardship.</u> If you found yourself facing a longer, harder winter than you anticipated, you could very possibly starve to death or freeze to death. If a train couldn’t get through with supplies, or if your crop hadn’t done as well as you hoped during the summer months, you could watch helplessly as your food and fuel stores dwindled down to nothing. Can you imagine watching your children starve and knowing there was nothing you could do? We live in a world where food can be delivered to our doorstep, where aid is much more easily attained for those who need financial assistance, where school lunches are provided for kids whose parents can’t afford them. <br /><br /><b>We live in a land of plenty.</b> Even though that same truth doesn’t stretch across the globe, there are people working on ensuring that no one, no matter where they live or how little they have, won’t have to go hungry. We have so much to be thankful for, and yet often we are so focused on the negatives that we forget to count our blessings. After reading this book, I’m thankful for so many things. I’m thankful for a well insulated house and a reliable furnace, and for a fire place and an unlimited amount of firewood should the power fail. I’m thankful for stocked cabinets and a full refrigerator and a freezer full of meat. I’m thankful for nonperishable food, for processed foods in cans and boxes and bags, even if I hope the day never comes when they’re all we have to eat. I’m thankful for my car and for paved roads. I’m thankful for grocery stores. I’m thankful that I will never have to endure the kind of winter that Laura and her family had to endure, and that starvation is incredibly unlikely unless I’m lost far away from home. <br /><br /><b>Honestly, I’m just thankful.</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="11165964461503894995" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_719755432" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/719755432" />
    <a title="Matthew Hunter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3040804-matthew-hunter"><img alt="Matthew Hunter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495642903p2/3040804.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/719755432?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew Hunter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew Hunter" href="/user/show/3040804-matthew-hunter">Matthew Hunter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780718805203" href="/book/show/77763.The_Long_Winter">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3040804-matthew-hunter?shelf=children-juvenile">children-juvenile</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3040804-matthew-hunter?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer719755432" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5212449242028516108">Wow. Some free advice from Uncle Matt - don't choose <i>The Long Winter</i> as a read along with your toddler while at the same time reading <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/46756.Oryx_and_Crake__MaddAddam_Trilogy___1_' title='Oryx and Crake (MaddAddam Trilogy, #1) by Margaret Atwood' rel='nofollow noopener'>Oryx and Crake</a>. Atwood's dystopian nightmare's challenge enough. Throw in a claustrophobic account of near-starvation during a long, cold, blizzard laden winter and it's almost too much to bear in combination.<br/><br/><i>The Long Winter</i>'s unlike any of the other books in the <i>Little House</i> series. The sense of foreboding and doom is palpable in the early chapters. Pa can't quit</span>
  <span id="freeText5212449242028516108" style="display:none">Wow. Some free advice from Uncle Matt - don't choose <i>The Long Winter</i> as a read along with your toddler while at the same time reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/46756.Oryx_and_Crake__MaddAddam_Trilogy___1_" title="Oryx and Crake (MaddAddam Trilogy, #1) by Margaret Atwood" rel="nofollow noopener">Oryx and Crake</a>. Atwood's dystopian nightmare's challenge enough. Throw in a claustrophobic account of near-starvation during a long, cold, blizzard laden winter and it's almost too much to bear in combination.<br /><br /><i>The Long Winter</i>'s unlike any of the other books in the <i>Little House</i> series. The sense of foreboding and doom is palpable in the early chapters. Pa can't quite put his finger on what's wrong with the weather pattern, but he's wary of what's ahead for the winter. And when a tribal elder shows up to say beware of the upcoming once-in-a-generation hard and long winter, the feeling of unease deepens even further. The constant blizzards start in October and last into April. Supply trains can't get through. Hunger and fights over dwindling resources ensue. Donner party anyone? Okay, there's no cannibalism. I've been reading too many post-apocalyptic/dystopian novels...<br /><br />To this point in the series, LIW has focused mainly on independence and freedom. The individual, or maybe the family unit, reigns supreme. Now that the Ingalls family is part of a newly formed town, <i>The Long Winter</i> explores the balance between the individual and the common good. Where does profit motive give way to community need? Is there a place for individual heroism in the public/common sphere? Can individuals and groups thrive together? Of course they can. The challenge is finding the sweet spot between individuation and cooperation. Easier said than done! But the stakes are high. Entire regions teetering on the brink of starvation; questions of international aid's role in feeding masses of hungry people; debates over the potential impact of privatizing water resources; providing our children a world class education - where should we draw the line between public and private initiative? If anyone tells you they have the one right answer, they're full of it. That said, we have no choice but to keep trying to find an appropriate balance at any given time. Whatever the case may be, LIW shows that individual generosity and gumption play essential roles in determining the common good.<br /><br />Yet another thought-provoking read along from LIW. Good stuff throughout this series!</span>
  <a data-text-id="5212449242028516108" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2521066077" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8524292557877139011">Another excellent read. I'm excited that Laura has finally met the Wilders. :D With all the blizzards and the family being stuck inside for the majority of the book, the story could have easily gotten boring or repetitive, but as always the author kept things interesting even if it isn't as lighthearted as it once was. I also once again appreciated seeing how they dealt with things like blizzards back then. Such a stark contrast to when we get blizzards today and get freaked out if the power eve</span>
  <span id="freeText8524292557877139011" style="display:none">Another excellent read. I'm excited that Laura has finally met the Wilders. :D With all the blizzards and the family being stuck inside for the majority of the book, the story could have easily gotten boring or repetitive, but as always the author kept things interesting even if it isn't as lighthearted as it once was. I also once again appreciated seeing how they dealt with things like blizzards back then. Such a stark contrast to when we get blizzards today and get freaked out if the power even goes off. :P The Ingalls always impress me with how resourceful and strong they are. Three more books in this series to go. :)<br /><br /><b>Content Advisory:</b><br /><br /><b>Violence/Peril:</b> Pa hunts in order to feed his family, but no animal deaths are described. There is some peril through much of the book related to the blizzards and the Ingalls family (and the whole town) worrying that they might not last the winter, but no one dies.<br /><br /><b>Sexual:</b> Laura is a teenager now and it's briefly hinted that an older boy might like her, but nothing untoward ever happens.</span>

<div id="review_1605732971" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1605732971" />
    <a title="David Schaafsma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma"><img alt="David Schaafsma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574997804p2/269235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1605732971?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Schaafsma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Schaafsma" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma">David Schaafsma</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer12982747565079172693">The family is finally done with this book, listening to Cherry Jones read it as we traveled over-the-rive-and-through-the-woods-to Grandmother's-house-we-go and over a few meals, even, and it was not always fun, sometimes tense, but on the whole it was good, as usual. <br/><br/>This one is mostly blizzards and near starvation from the South Dakota winter. Tedious, for a while, then realistically and impressively oppressive and frightening. They could actually have starved. They go months never eating in a</span>
  <span id="freeText12982747565079172693" style="display:none">The family is finally done with this book, listening to Cherry Jones read it as we traveled over-the-rive-and-through-the-woods-to Grandmother's-house-we-go and over a few meals, even, and it was not always fun, sometimes tense, but on the whole it was good, as usual. <br /><br />This one is mostly blizzards and near starvation from the South Dakota winter. Tedious, for a while, then realistically and impressively oppressive and frightening. They could actually have starved. They go months never eating in any given day what might be required for ONE full meal! If some of the volumes are a little sugar-coated (by that eternally optimistic, always-look-on-the-bright-side-of-life-mother, this one focused on the border of long winters, the hard physical labor of grinding wheat into grain and the desperation to survive. I liked getting a pretty good picture of that, with the kids, so we could talk about world hunger.</span>

<div id="review_3240065319" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3240065319" />
    <a title="Deb (Readerbuzz) Nance" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1654645-deb-readerbuzz-nance"><img alt="Deb (Readerbuzz) Nance" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1361048508p2/1654645.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3240065319?book_show_action=true">Mar 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deb (Readerbuzz) Nance" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deb (Readerbuzz) Nance" href="/user/show/1654645-deb-readerbuzz-nance">Deb (Readerbuzz) Nance</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1654645-deb-readerbuzz-nance?shelf=children-s-books">children-s-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1654645-deb-readerbuzz-nance?shelf=family">family</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1654645-deb-readerbuzz-nance?shelf=community">community</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1654645-deb-readerbuzz-nance?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3240065319" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9752420280045544102">I must have started and set aside a half-dozen books before I settled into The Long Winter. I'm finding it difficult to read in these uncertain times, but I saw Ruth recommending Long Winter on her blog <a href='https://www.greatbookstudy.com/2020/03/do-you-need-perspective-during-these.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Great Book Study</a> this week and I decided to give this book a try.<br/><br/>I'm very glad I did. If you are feeling worried and anxious, The Long Winter is a good book to read.<br/><br/>The Long Winter is the true story of the Ingalls family during a terrible season of blizzards that extended from October to late A</span>
  <span id="freeText9752420280045544102" style="display:none">I must have started and set aside a half-dozen books before I settled into The Long Winter. I'm finding it difficult to read in these uncertain times, but I saw Ruth recommending Long Winter on her blog <a href="https://www.greatbookstudy.com/2020/03/do-you-need-perspective-during-these.html" rel="nofollow noopener">A Great Book Study</a> this week and I decided to give this book a try.<br /><br />I'm very glad I did. If you are feeling worried and anxious, The Long Winter is a good book to read.<br /><br />The Long Winter is the true story of the Ingalls family during a terrible season of blizzards that extended from October to late April. The Ingalls moved into town so they would have sturdy quarters as well as access to the supplies the train would bring through the winter. Deep into the winter, they learned the train would not be arriving, and there would be no new supplies. People were terribly worried as that would mean no way to stay warm and no food. <br /><br />Reading about the way the family and the community worked together to help each other was helpful for me. It was also useful to put our current crisis into perspective. And it was somehow reassuring to see that not everyone was able to act in ways that put the needs of the group ahead of the needs of the individual; this was no whitewashed version of the situation. <br /><br />This is historical fiction, and the attitudes and prejudices of the times are part of this story, so be prepared for that. <br /><br />But, in spite of these, The Long Winter is a good story for these times, I think. It reminds me that people have always faced terrible situations, and that we must work together to overcome them, putting the needs of the group before our own selfish interests.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9752420280045544102" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I agree with one of Kim Stanley Robinson's characters in the Science in the Capitol series, who suggested this might be the great American novel.</span>

I don't think anyone in this country really can anymore.<br /><br />I read this aloud to my husband and kids this fall.               >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8362158966861346785"><strong>Review #1</strong> - The Little House series was so popular in my school in 1975 that after I’d finished <i><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77767.Little_House_on_the_Prairie__Little_House___2_' title='Little House on the Prairie (Little House, #2) by Laura Ingalls Wilder' rel='nofollow noopener'>Little House on the Prairie</a>,</i> the only book available in my school library was the sixth in the series, <i>The Long Winter</i>. At 400+ pages, it was the longest book I’d ever read, and it took me months. Kids in my class even commented about it. “It’s called <i>The Long Winter</i> because it’s long book.” And that was one of the more neutral comments. Much more typical was, “You’re <i>still</i> reading that?” And from the</span>
  <span id="freeText8362158966861346785" style="display:none"><strong>Review #1</strong> - The Little House series was so popular in my school in 1975 that after I’d finished <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77767.Little_House_on_the_Prairie__Little_House___2_" title="Little House on the Prairie (Little House, #2) by Laura Ingalls Wilder" rel="nofollow noopener">Little House on the Prairie</a>,</i> the only book available in my school library was the sixth in the series, <i>The Long Winter</i>. At 400+ pages, it was the longest book I’d ever read, and it took me months. Kids in my class even commented about it. “It’s called <i>The Long Winter</i> because it’s long book.” And that was one of the more neutral comments. Much more typical was, “You’re <i>still</i> reading that?” And from the teacher’s pet: “I finished it in four days.” And so, though I’d been best reader in my class in Manhattan, in my new school in Queens, I acquired a reputation for being a slow reader and therefore, a dumb kid.<br /><br />What I wish someone had told me back then was that <i>The Long Winter</i> was not meant for a girl of seven. Laura is about thirteen in the book, so the ideal reader is her age or close to it. I’d say the first four books, <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8337.Little_House_in_the_Big_Woods__Little_House___1_" title="Little House in the Big Woods (Little House, #1) by Laura Ingalls Wilder" rel="nofollow noopener">Little House in the Big Woods</a>,</i> <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77767.Little_House_on_the_Prairie__Little_House___2_" title="Little House on the Prairie (Little House, #2) by Laura Ingalls Wilder" rel="nofollow noopener">Little House on the Prairie</a>,</i> <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8252.Farmer_Boy__Little_House___3_" title="Farmer Boy (Little House, #3) by Laura Ingalls Wilder" rel="nofollow noopener">Farmer Boy</a>,</i> and <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7882.On_the_Banks_of_Plum_Creek__Little_House___4_" title="On the Banks of Plum Creek (Little House, #4) by Laura Ingalls Wilder" rel="nofollow noopener">On the Banks of Plum Creek</a>,</i> are perfect for kids from grades two through four. Everything from <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8248.By_the_Shores_of_Silver_Lake__Little_House___5_" title="By the Shores of Silver Lake (Little House, #5) by Laura Ingalls Wilder" rel="nofollow noopener">By the Shores of Silver Lake</a>
</i> and afterward is for fifth or sixth graders at least.<br /><br />So I had some pretty painful associations with this book, and I didn’t remember much of it. I remember being struck at how Carrie was no longer “Baby Carrie,” but a kid who spent more time with Laura than Mary, who, of course, had gone blind. I don’t remember my reactions to that at all, though it may be that another fan clued me in on how it happened. I do remember the Wilder boys, and of course, the main theme – the big blizzard that kept everyone stranded for months.<br /><br />When that kid in my class said, “It’s called <i>The Long Winter</i> because it’s a long book,” I took it as him making fun of my stupidity. Now, I see it differently. Perhaps I don’t remember the details of the plot, but I <strong>felt</strong> <i>The Long Winter</i>. It was hard, it took months, I couldn’t wait for it to end, and somehow, I got through it.<br /><br /><strong>Review #2</strong> - Okay, update since I wrote the above. I re-read <i>The Long Winter</i> in its entirety over Passover, and much of it out loud to my son. He's a teenager who's perfectly capable of reading to himself, but hey, I'll do anything to connect to him at this rough stage. Anyway, we both loved it. It may just be my favorite Little House book of all. And now that I'm learning about Rose Wilder Lane's hand in the books and her libertarian ideology, I couldn't help but look at the book in that light. I've started a discussion, with quotes, at this link: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/548395">http://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/5...</a><br /><br />Also, I'm not quite sure how I feel about what Almanzo did. I mean, obviously it was heroic, but he got someone else to sell what he wasn't willing to sell himself. It doesn't seem entirely pure. But my husband says that if the guy wouldn't have sold, and then Almanzo refused to sell, then we could judge his actions as wrong. But given the sacrifices he made, one would think that if push came to shove, he would have sold for the sake of the starving community. Any thoughts? </span>
  <a data-text-id="8362158966861346785" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15938802685986831562">Wow! What a story. I'm almost glad that I never read this when I was younger. I don't think I would have appreciated the magnitude of what they had to live through. And it meant so much more knowing that the experiences described here were based on actual events. This definitely deserves its Newbery honor, and I'm VERY glad that I finally got around to reading it! I never thought any of the other books in the series would come close to eclipsing my love for <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77766.Little_House_in_the_Big_Woods__Little_House___1_' title='Little House in the Big Woods (Little House, #1) by Laura Ingalls Wilder' rel='nofollow noopener'>Little House in the Big Woods</a> which I </span>
  <span id="freeText15938802685986831562" style="display:none">Wow! What a story. I'm almost glad that I never read this when I was younger. I don't think I would have appreciated the magnitude of what they had to live through. And it meant so much more knowing that the experiences described here were based on actual events. This definitely deserves its Newbery honor, and I'm VERY glad that I finally got around to reading it! I never thought any of the other books in the series would come close to eclipsing my love for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77766.Little_House_in_the_Big_Woods__Little_House___1_" title="Little House in the Big Woods (Little House, #1) by Laura Ingalls Wilder" rel="nofollow noopener">Little House in the Big Woods</a> which I read over and over as a child, but I think this one might have done it. It's a story that makes you cold, but also very grateful for the blessings you take for granted that surround you every day. It also makes me think wistfully of living a simpler life. And now I look forward to reading the next in the series where I know Almanzo again plays an important part in the story!<br /><br />A favorite quote: <br />"If only I had some grease I could fix some kind of a light," Ma considered. "We didn't lack for light when I was a girl before this newfangled kerosene was ever heard of."<br /><br />"That's so," said Pa. "These times are too progressive. Everything has changed too fast. Railroads and telegraph and kerosene and coal stoves--they're good things to have, but the trouble is, folks get to depend on 'em." <br /><br />Note: I'm shelving this series as both historical fiction and autobiography since it really is a bit of both. </span>
